Seats</a></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;"><div style="width: 100px;"><span class="nowrap">14 / 91</span><div role="img" style="box-sizing: border-box; border: 1px solid #aaa; height: 1.15em; position: relative;"><div style="background-color: #E81B23; width: 15%; height: 100%;"></div></div></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height: 0.8em; padding-top:0.5em; padding-bottom:0;">Website</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height:1.3em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.gop.com/">gop.com</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;padding-top:0;"><div class="plainlist" style="line-height:1.3em; padding-top:0.3em; border-top:2px solid #E81B23;"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Politics_of_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Politics of United States">Politics of United States</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="List of political parties in United States">Political parties</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Elections_in_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Elections in United States">Elections</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Republican Party</b>, also referred to as the <b>GOP</b> (<b>Grand Old Party</b>), is one of the <a href="/wiki/Two-party_system" title="Two-party system">two</a> <a href="/wiki/Major_party" title="Major party">major</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_parties_in_the_United_States" title="Political parties in the United States">political parties in the United States</a>; the other is its historic rival, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>.
</p><p>The GOP was founded in 1854 by opponents of the <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas–Nebraska Act">Kansas–Nebraska Act</a>, which allowed for the potential expansion of slavery into certain U.S. territories. The party supported <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberalism</a>, opposed the expansion of slavery, and supported <a href="/wiki/Economic_reform" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic reform">economic reform</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> was the first Republican president. Under the leadership of Lincoln and a Republican Congress, slavery was banned in the United States in 1865. The Party was generally dominant during the <a href="/wiki/Third_Party_System" title="Third Party System">Third Party System</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Party_System" title="Fourth Party System">Fourth Party System</a>. After 1912, the Party underwent an ideological shift to the right.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a>, the party's core base shifted, with <a href="/wiki/Southern_strategy" title="Southern strategy">Southern states</a> becoming more reliably Republican in presidential politics.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Since the 1990s, the Party's support has chiefly come from the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountain States">Mountain States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rural_areas_in_the_United_States" title="Rural areas in the United States">rural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exurb" title="Exurb">exurban areas</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Midwest_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Midwest United States">Midwest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 21st century Republican Party ideology is <a href="/wiki/American_conservatism" class="mw-redirect" title="American conservatism">American conservatism</a>. The GOP supports lower taxes, <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>, a strong national defense, <a href="/wiki/Right_to_keep_and_bear_arms" title="Right to keep and bear arms">gun rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-abortion_movement" title="Anti-abortion movement">pro-life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> and restrictions on <a href="/wiki/Labor_unions_in_the_United_States" title="Labor unions in the United States">labor unions</a>. In addition to advocating for <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">conservative economic policies</a>, the Republican Party is <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Social conservatism in the United States">socially conservative</a>. After the Supreme Court's 1973 decision in <i><a href="/wiki/Roe_v._Wade" title="Roe v. Wade">Roe v. Wade</a></i>, the Republican Party opposed abortion in its party platform and grew its support among <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism_in_the_United_States" title="Evangelicalism in the United States">evangelicals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Great_Divide:_Religious_and_Cultural_Conflict_in_American_Party_Politics2_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Great_Divide:_Religious_and_Cultural_Conflict_in_American_Party_Politics2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The GOP was strongly committed to protectionism and tariffs at its founding, but grew more supportive of <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> in the 20th century.
</p><p>There have been 19 Republican presidents, the most from any one political party (including current president <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>, who was elected in 2016). As of 2019, the GOP controls the presidency, a majority in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Senate">U.S. Senate</a>, a majority of state governorships, a majority (30) of state legislatures, and 22 state <a href="/wiki/Government_trifecta" title="Government trifecta">government trifectas</a> (governorship and both legislative chambers). Five of the nine sitting <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Supreme Court">U.S. Supreme Court</a> justices were nominated by Republican presidents.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party" title="History of the United States Republican Party">History of the United States Republican Party</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Third_Party_System" title="Third Party System">Third Party System</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/170px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/255px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/340px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2850" data-file-height="3742" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, first Republican president (1861–1865)</div></div></div>
<p>Founded in the Northern states in 1854 by <a href="/wiki/Abolitionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Abolitionist">abolitionists</a>, ex-<a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whigs</a>, and ex-<a href="/wiki/Free_Soil" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Soil">Free Soilers</a>, the Republican Party quickly became the principal opposition to the dominant <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> and the briefly popular <a href="/wiki/Know_Nothing" title="Know Nothing">Know Nothing Party</a>. The party grew out of opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas–Nebraska Act">Kansas–Nebraska Act</a>, which repealed the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> and opened <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Territory" title="Kansas Territory">Kansas Territory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nebraska_Territory" title="Nebraska Territory">Nebraska Territory</a> to slavery and future admission as slave states.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The Republicans called for economic and social <a href="/wiki/Modernization" class="mw-redirect" title="Modernization">modernization</a>. They denounced the expansion of slavery as a great evil, but did not call for ending it in the Southern states. The first public meeting of the general <a href="/wiki/Anti-Nebraska_movement" title="Anti-Nebraska movement">anti-Nebraska movement</a>, at which the name Republican was proposed, was held on March 20, 1854 in <a href="/wiki/Ripon,_Wisconsin" title="Ripon, Wisconsin">Ripon, Wisconsin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The name was partly chosen to pay homage to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Republican Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The first official party convention was held on July 6, 1854 in <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Michigan" title="Jackson, Michigan">Jackson, Michigan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the 1856 Republican National Convention, the party adopted a national platform emphasizing opposition to the expansion of slavery into U.S. territories.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> While Republican candidate <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a> lost the <a href="/wiki/1856_United_States_presidential_election" title="1856 United States presidential election">1856 United States presidential election</a> to James Buchanan, he did win 11 of the 16 northern states.<sup id="cite_ref-auto4_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CharlesRJennison.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dc/CharlesRJennison.jpg/170px-CharlesRJennison.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dc/CharlesRJennison.jpg/255px-CharlesRJennison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dc/CharlesRJennison.jpg/340px-CharlesRJennison.jpg 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="830" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CharlesRJennison.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Jennison" title="Charles R. Jennison">Charles R. Jennison</a>, an anti-slavery militia leader associated with the <a href="/wiki/Jayhawkers" class="mw-redirect" title="Jayhawkers">Jayhawkers</a> from <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a> and an early Republican politician in the region.</div></div></div>
<p>The Republican Party first came to power in <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_1860" class="mw-redirect" title="United States elections, 1860">the elections of 1860</a> when it won control of both houses of Congress and its candidate, former congressman <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, was elected President. In the <a href="/wiki/1864_United_States_presidential_election" title="1864 United States presidential election">election of 1864</a>, it united with <a href="/wiki/War_Democrats" class="mw-redirect" title="War Democrats">War Democrats</a> to nominate Lincoln on the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_Party_(United_States)" title="National Union Party (United States)">National Union Party</a> ticket;<sup id="cite_ref-auto4_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Lincoln won re-election.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Under Republican congressional leadership, the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a>—which banned slavery in the United States—passed the Senate in 1864 and the House in 1865; it was ratified in December 1865.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_1870-1880.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ulysses_S._Grant_1870-1880.jpg/170px-Ulysses_S._Grant_1870-1880.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ulysses_S._Grant_1870-1880.jpg/255px-Ulysses_S._Grant_1870-1880.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ulysses_S._Grant_1870-1880.jpg/340px-Ulysses_S._Grant_1870-1880.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_1870-1880.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, 18th President of the United States (1869–1877)</div></div></div>
<p>The party's success created factionalism within the party in the 1870s. Those who believed that Reconstruction had been accomplished, and was continued mostly to promote the large-scale corruption tolerated by President <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, ran <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a> for the presidency. The <a href="/wiki/Stalwarts_(politics)" title="Stalwarts (politics)">Stalwart</a> faction defended Grant and the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a>, whereas the <a href="/wiki/Half-Breed_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Half-Breed (politics)">Half-Breeds</a> pushed for reform of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_service" title="Civil service">civil service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pendleton_Civil_Service_Reform_Act" title="Pendleton Civil Service Reform Act">Pendleton Civil Service Reform Act</a> was passed in 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Republican Party supported hard money (i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>), <a href="/wiki/Tariffs_in_American_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Tariffs in American history">high tariffs to promote economic growth, high wages and high profits</a>, generous pensions for Union veterans, and (after 1893) the annexation of <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>. The Republicans had strong support from <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">pietistic</a> Protestants, but they resisted demands for <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">Prohibition</a>. As the Northern postwar economy boomed with heavy and light industry, railroads, mines, fast-growing cities, and prosperous agriculture, the Republicans took credit and promoted policies to sustain the fast growth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The GOP was usually dominant over the Democrats during the Third Party System (1850s–1890s). However, by 1890 the Republicans had agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Antitrust_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Sherman Antitrust Act">Sherman Antitrust Act</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Commerce_Commission" title="Interstate Commerce Commission">Interstate Commerce Commission</a> in response to complaints from owners of small businesses and farmers. The high <a href="/wiki/McKinley_Tariff" title="McKinley Tariff">McKinley Tariff</a> of 1890 hurt the party and the Democrats swept to a landslide in the off-year elections, even defeating McKinley himself. The Democrats elected <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> in 1884 and 1892. The election of <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> in <a href="/wiki/1896_United_States_presidential_election" title="1896 United States presidential election">1896</a> was marked by a resurgence of Republican dominance that lasted (except for 1912 and 1916) until 1932. McKinley promised that high tariffs would end the severe hardship caused by the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">Panic of 1893</a> and that Republicans would guarantee a sort of pluralism in which all groups would benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Republican Civil War era program included free homestead farms, a federally subsidized transcontinental railroad, a national banking system, a large national debt, land grants for higher education, a new national banking system, a wartime income tax and permanent high tariffs to promote industrial growth and high wages. By the 1870s, they had adopted as well a hard money system based on the gold standard and fought off efforts to promote inflation through <a href="/wiki/Free_Silver" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Silver">Free Silver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> They created the foundations of the modern welfare state through an extensive program of pensions for Union veterans.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Foreign-policy issues were rarely a matter of partisan dispute, but briefly in the 1893–1904 period the GOP supported imperialistic expansion regarding Hawaii, the Philippines and the Panama Canal.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Party_System" title="Fourth Party System">Fourth Party System</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Theodore_Roosevelt,_1904.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/President_Theodore_Roosevelt%2C_1904.jpg/170px-President_Theodore_Roosevelt%2C_1904.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/President_Theodore_Roosevelt%2C_1904.jpg/255px-President_Theodore_Roosevelt%2C_1904.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/President_Theodore_Roosevelt%2C_1904.jpg/340px-President_Theodore_Roosevelt%2C_1904.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2842" data-file-height="3751" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Theodore_Roosevelt,_1904.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, 26th President of the United States (1901–1909)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Hoover_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/President_Hoover_portrait.jpg/170px-President_Hoover_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/President_Hoover_portrait.jpg/255px-President_Hoover_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/President_Hoover_portrait.jpg/340px-President_Hoover_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1087" data-file-height="1448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Hoover_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>, 31st President of the United States (1929–1933)</div></div></div>
<p>The 1896 realignment cemented the Republicans as the party of big business while <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> added more small business support by his embrace of <a href="/wiki/Trust_busting" class="mw-redirect" title="Trust busting">trust busting</a>. He handpicked his successor <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> in 1908, but they became enemies as the party split down the middle. Taft defeated Roosevelt for the 1912 nomination and Roosevelt ran on the ticket of his new <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(United_States,_1912)" title="Progressive Party (United States, 1912)">Progressive ("Bull Moose") Party</a>. He called for <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">social reforms</a>, many of which were later championed by <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_Coalition" class="mw-redirect" title="New Deal Coalition">New Deal Democrats</a> in the 1930s. He lost and when most of his supporters returned to the GOP they found they did not agree with the new <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative economic thinking</a>, leading to an ideological shift to the right in the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The Republicans returned to the White House throughout the 1920s, running on platforms of normalcy, business-oriented efficiency and high tariffs. The national party platform avoided mention of prohibition, instead issuing a vague commitment to law and order.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> were resoundingly elected in <a href="/wiki/1920_United_States_presidential_election" title="1920 United States presidential election">1920</a>, <a href="/wiki/1924_United_States_presidential_election" title="1924 United States presidential election">1924</a> and <a href="/wiki/1928_United_States_presidential_election" title="1928 United States presidential election">1928</a> respectively. The <a href="/wiki/Teapot_Dome_scandal" title="Teapot Dome scandal">Teapot Dome scandal</a> threatened to hurt the party but Harding died and Coolidge blamed everything on him as the opposition splintered in 1924. The pro-business policies of the decade seemed to produce an unprecedented prosperity until the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">Wall Street Crash of 1929</a> heralded the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Deal_era">New Deal era</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Old_Right_(United_States)" title="Old Right (United States)">Old Right (United States)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Party_System" title="Fifth Party System">Fifth Party System</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party#Fighting_the_New_Deal_Coalition:_1932–1980" title="History of the United States Republican Party">History of the United States Republican Party §&#160;Fighting the New Deal Coalition: 1932–1980</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_official_photo_portrait,_May_29,_1959.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_photo_portrait%2C_May_29%2C_1959.jpg/170px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_photo_portrait%2C_May_29%2C_1959.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_photo_portrait%2C_May_29%2C_1959.jpg/255px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_photo_portrait%2C_May_29%2C_1959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_photo_portrait%2C_May_29%2C_1959.jpg/340px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_photo_portrait%2C_May_29%2C_1959.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2782" data-file-height="3484" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_official_photo_portrait,_May_29,_1959.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a>, 34th President of the United States (1953–1961)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_coalition" title="New Deal coalition">New Deal coalition</a> of Democrat Franklin D. Roosevelt controlled American politics for most of the next three decades, excepting the two-term presidency of Republican <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>. Blacks moved into the Democratic Party during the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> era as they could vote in the North, but not in the South. After Roosevelt took office in 1933, New Deal legislation sailed through Congress and the economy moved sharply upward from its nadir in early 1933. However, long-term unemployment remained a drag until 1940. In the 1934 midterm elections, 10 Republican senators went down to defeat, leaving them with only 25 against 71 Democrats. The House of Representatives likewise had overwhelming Democratic majorities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The Republican Party split into a majority "Old Right" (based in the Midwest) and a liberal wing based in the Northeast that supported much of the New Deal. The Old Right sharply attacked the "Second New Deal" and said it represented class warfare and socialism. Roosevelt was re-elected in a landslide in 1936, but as his second term began the economy declined, strikes soared and he failed to take control of the Supreme Court or to purge the Southern conservatives in the Democratic Party. Republicans made a major comeback in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1938" class="mw-redirect" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1938">1938 elections</a> and had new rising stars such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert A. Taft</a> of Ohio on the right and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Dewey" title="Thomas E. Dewey">Thomas E. Dewey</a> of New York on the left.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Southern conservatives joined with most Republicans to form the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_coalition" title="Conservative coalition">conservative coalition</a>, which dominated domestic issues in Congress until 1964. Both parties split on foreign policy issues, with the anti-war isolationists dominant in the Republican Party and the interventionists who wanted to stop <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> dominant in the Democratic Party. Roosevelt won a third and fourth term in 1940 and 1944. Conservatives abolished most of the New Deal during the war, but they did not attempt to reverse Social Security or the agencies that regulated business.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>
Historian <a href="/wiki/George_H._Nash" title="George H. Nash">George H. Nash</a> argues: </p><blockquote><p>Unlike the "moderate", internationalist, largely eastern bloc of Republicans who accepted (or at least acquiesced in) some of the "Roosevelt Revolution" and the essential premises of President Truman's foreign policy, the Republican Right at heart was counterrevolutionary. Anti-collectivist, anti-Communist, anti-New Deal, passionately committed to limited government, free market economics, and congressional (as opposed to executive) prerogatives, the G.O.P. conservatives were obliged from the start to wage a constant two-front war: against liberal Democrats from without and "me-too" Republicans from within.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The Democrats elected majorities to Congress almost continuously after 1932 (the GOP won only in 1946 and 1952), but the conservative coalition blocked practically all major liberal proposals in domestic policy. After 1945, the internationalist wing of the GOP cooperated with Harry S. Truman's <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> foreign policy, funded the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> and supported NATO, despite the continued isolationism of the Old Right.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_M._Nixon,_ca._1935_-_1982_-_NARA_-_530679.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Richard_M._Nixon%2C_ca._1935_-_1982_-_NARA_-_530679.jpg/170px-Richard_M._Nixon%2C_ca._1935_-_1982_-_NARA_-_530679.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Richard_M._Nixon%2C_ca._1935_-_1982_-_NARA_-_530679.jpg/255px-Richard_M._Nixon%2C_ca._1935_-_1982_-_NARA_-_530679.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Richard_M._Nixon%2C_ca._1935_-_1982_-_NARA_-_530679.jpg/340px-Richard_M._Nixon%2C_ca._1935_-_1982_-_NARA_-_530679.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2539" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_M._Nixon,_ca._1935_-_1982_-_NARA_-_530679.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, 37th President of the United States (1969–1974)</div></div></div>
<p>The second half of the 20th century saw election or succession of Republican presidents <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>. Eisenhower had defeated conservative leader Senator Robert A. Taft for the 1952 nomination, but conservatives dominated the domestic policies of the Eisenhower administration. Voters liked Eisenhower much more than they liked the GOP and he proved unable to shift the party to a more moderate position. Since 1976, liberalism has virtually faded out of the Republican Party, apart from a few Northeastern holdouts.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicol_C._Rae_1989_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicol_C._Rae_1989-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Official_Portrait_of_President_Reagan_1981.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Official_Portrait_of_President_Reagan_1981.jpg/170px-Official_Portrait_of_President_Reagan_1981.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Official_Portrait_of_President_Reagan_1981.jpg/255px-Official_Portrait_of_President_Reagan_1981.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Official_Portrait_of_President_Reagan_1981.jpg/340px-Official_Portrait_of_President_Reagan_1981.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2399" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Official_Portrait_of_President_Reagan_1981.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, 40th President of the United States (1981–1989)</div></div></div>
<p>Ever since he left office in 1989, Reagan has been the iconic conservative Republican and Republican presidential candidates frequently claim to share his views and aim to establish themselves and their policies as the more appropriate heir to his legacy.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1994, the party, led by House Minority Whip <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a> campaigning on the "<a href="/wiki/Contract_with_America" title="Contract with America">Contract with America</a>", was elected to majorities in both Houses of Congress during the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Revolution" title="Republican Revolution">Republican Revolution</a>. However, as House Speaker Gingrich was unable to deliver on much of its promises, including a balanced-budget amendment and term limits for members of Congress. During the <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_and_acquittal_of_President_Bill_Clinton" class="mw-redirect" title="Impeachment and acquittal of President Bill Clinton">impeachment and acquittal of President Bill Clinton</a>, Republicans suffered surprise losses in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1998" class="mw-redirect" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1998">1998 midterm elections</a>; Gingrich's popularity sank to 17%; he took the blame and resigned.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span></h3>
<p>The Senate majority lasted until 2001 when the Senate became split evenly, but it was regained in the 2002 elections. Both Republican majorities in the House and Senate were held until the Democrats regained control in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="United States elections, 2006">mid-term elections of 2006</a>. The Republican Party has since been defined by <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Social conservatism in the United States">social conservatism</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Preemptive_war" title="Preemptive war">preemptive war</a> foreign policy intended to defeat terrorism and promote global democracy, a more powerful <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">executive branch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supply_side_economics" class="mw-redirect" title="Supply side economics">supply side economics</a>, support for gun ownership and deregulation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, 41st President of the United States (1989–1993)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:165px;max-width:165px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:George-W-Bush.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/George-W-Bush.jpeg/163px-George-W-Bush.jpeg" decoding="async" width="163" height="216" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/George-W-Bush.jpeg/245px-George-W-Bush.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/George-W-Bush.jpeg/326px-George-W-Bush.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2267" data-file-height="3000" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>, 43rd President of the United States (2001–2009)</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Former president George H. W. Bush was the father of former president George W. Bush. The son of a president has only been elected president twice in United States history (President <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> was the son of <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, the second president of the United States).</div></div></div></div>
<p>A George W. Bush-Dick Cheney ticket won the 2000 and 2004 presidential elections. In the <a href="/wiki/2008_United_States_presidential_election" title="2008 United States presidential election">presidential election of 2008</a>, the <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>-<a href="/wiki/Sarah_Palin" title="Sarah Palin">Sarah Palin</a> ticket was defeated by Senator <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>.
</p><p>2010 was a year of electoral success for the Republicans, starting with the upset win of <a href="/wiki/Scott_Brown_(politician)" title="Scott Brown (politician)">Scott Brown</a> in the Massachusetts special Senate election for the seat held for many decades by the Democratic Kennedy brothers. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2010" class="mw-redirect" title="United States elections, 2010">November elections</a>, Republicans recaptured control of the House, increased their number of seats in the Senate and gained a majority of governorships.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Obama won <a href="/wiki/2012_United_States_presidential_election" title="2012 United States presidential election">re-election in 2012</a>, defeating a Mitt Romney-Paul Ryan ticket. The campaign focused largely on the <a href="/wiki/Patient_Protection_and_Affordable_Care_Act" title="Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act">Affordable Care Act</a> and President Obama's stewardship of the economy, as the country still faced high unemployment numbers and a rising national debt stemming from the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession_in_the_United_States" title="Great Recession in the United States">Great Recession</a>. While Republicans lost 7 seats in the House in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2012" class="mw-redirect" title="United States elections, 2012">November congressional elections</a>, they still retained control. However, Republicans were not able to gain control of the Senate, continuing their minority status with a net loss of two seats.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In the aftermath of the loss, some prominent Republicans spoke out against their own party.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> A post-2012 post-mortem report by the Republican Party concluded that the party needed to do more on the national level to attract votes from minorities and young voters.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2014" class="mw-redirect" title="United States elections, 2014">2014 midterm elections</a>, the Republican Party took control of the Senate by gaining nine seats.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> With a final total of 247 seats (57%) in the House and 54 seats in the Senate, the Republicans ultimately achieved their largest majority in the Congress since the <a href="/wiki/71st_United_States_Congress" title="71st United States Congress">71st Congress</a> in 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Donald_Trump_official_portrait_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Donald_Trump_official_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Donald_Trump_official_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Donald_Trump_official_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Donald_Trump_official_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Donald_Trump_official_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Donald_Trump_official_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1520" data-file-height="2096" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Donald_Trump_official_portrait_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>, 45th and current President of the United States (2017–present)</div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2016" class="mw-redirect" title="United States elections, 2016">2016 elections</a>, Republicans maintained a majority in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_elections,_2016" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate elections, 2016">Senate</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_2016" class="mw-redirect" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 2016">House</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_gubernatorial_elections,_2016" class="mw-redirect" title="United States gubernatorial elections, 2016">Governorships</a> and elected <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> as <a href="/wiki/2016_United_States_presidential_election" title="2016 United States presidential election">President</a>. The Republican Party controlled 69 of 99 state legislative chambers in 2017, the most it had held in history;<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and at least 33 governorships, the most it had held since 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The party had total control of government (legislative chambers and governorship) in 25 states,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> the most since 1952;<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> the opposing Democratic Party had full control in only five states.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2019, there have been a total of 19 Republican Presidents (the most from any one party in American history). Republicans have won 24 of the last 40 presidential elections.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Following the results of the <a href="/wiki/2018_midterm_elections" class="mw-redirect" title="2018 midterm elections">2018 midterm elections</a>, the Republican Party controls the bulk of the power in the United States as of 2019, holding the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Executive_Branch" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Executive Branch">presidency</a> (Donald Trump), a majority in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a>, and a majority of <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_governors" title="List of United States governors">governorships</a> (27) and <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_state_legislatures" title="List of United States state legislatures">state legislatures</a> (full control of 30/50 legislatures, split control of two).<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> As of 2019, the GOP holds a "<a href="/wiki/Government_trifecta" title="Government trifecta">trifecta</a>" (control of the executive branch and both chambers of the legislative branch) in a plurality of states (22 of 50).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Five of the nine current justices of the Supreme Court were appointed by Republican presidents.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_trends">Recent trends</span></h4>
<p>For most of the post-World War II era, Republicans had little presence at the state legislative level. This trend began to reverse in the late 1990s, with Republicans increasing their state legislative presence and taking control of state legislatures in the South. From 2004 to 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Republican_State_Leadership_Committee" title="Republican State Leadership Committee">Republican State Leadership Committee</a> (RSLC) raised over $140 million targeted to state legislature races, while the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Legislative_Campaign_Committee" title="Democratic Legislative Campaign Committee">Democratic Legislative Campaign Committee</a> (DLSC) raised less than half that during that time period. Following the 2014 midterm elections, Republicans controlled 68 of 98 partisan state legislative houses (the most in the party's history) and controlled both the executive and legislative branches of government in 24 states (Democrats had control of only seven).<sup id="cite_ref-legislatures_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legislatures-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the inauguration of Republican <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> as President, the Republican Party remained fairly cohesive for much of the 2000s as both strong <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic libertarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Social conservatism in the United States">social conservatives</a> opposed the Democrats, whom they saw as the party of bloated, secular, and liberal government.<sup id="cite_ref-Wooldridge_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wooldridge-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The Bush-era rise of what were known as "pro-government conservatives"—a core part of the President's base—meant that a considerable group of the Republicans advocated for increased government spending and greater regulations covering both the economy and people's personal lives as well as for an activist, <a href="/wiki/Interventionism_(politics)" title="Interventionism (politics)">interventionist foreign policy</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Survey groups such as the Pew Research Center found that social conservatives and free market advocates remained the other two main groups within the party's coalition of support, with all three being roughly equal in number.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Republican" title="Libertarian Republican">libertarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_conservatism" title="Libertarian conservatism">libertarian-leaning conservatives</a> increasingly found fault with what they saw as Republicans' restricting of vital <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties_in_the_United_States" title="Civil liberties in the United States">civil liberties</a> while <a href="/wiki/Corporate_welfare" title="Corporate welfare">corporate welfare</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_national_debt" class="mw-redirect" title="United States national debt">national debt</a> hiked considerably under Bush's tenure.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, some social conservatives expressed dissatisfaction with the party's support for economic policies that conflicted with their moral values.<sup id="cite_ref-scare_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scare-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2013, National Committee Chairman <a href="/wiki/Reince_Priebus" title="Reince Priebus">Reince Priebus</a> gave a stinging report on the party's electoral failures in 2012, calling on Republicans to reinvent themselves and officially endorse immigration reform. He said: "There's no one reason we lost. Our message was weak; our ground game was insufficient; we weren't inclusive; we were behind in both data and digital, and our primary and debate process needed improvement". He proposed 219 reforms that included a $10 million marketing campaign to reach women, minorities and gays as well as setting a shorter, more controlled primary season and creating better data collection facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With a majority of Republicans and Republican-leaning independents under the age of 49 supporting <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_the_United_States" title="Same-sex marriage in the United States">legal recognition of same-sex marriages</a> versus the opposition remaining from those over 50, the issue remained a divisive one within the party. Former House Speaker <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a> remarked that the "[p]arty is going to be torn on this issue".<sup id="cite_ref-gay-mar-sup_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gay-mar-sup-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> A Reuters/Ipsos survey from April 2015 found that 68% of Americans overall would attend the same-sex wedding of a loved one, with 56% of Republicans agreeing. Reuters journalist Jeff Mason remarked that "Republicans who stake out strong opposition to gay marriage could be on shaky political ground if their ultimate goal is to win the White House" given the divide between the social conservative stalwarts and the rest of the United States that opposes them.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, the Supreme Court of the United States ruled bans on same-sex marriage to be unconstitutional, thus legalizing same-sex marriage nationwide.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, after being elected President, Republican Donald Trump stated that he was "fine" with same-sex marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-auto7_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_symbols">Name and symbols</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:NastRepublicanElephant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/NastRepublicanElephant.jpg/220px-NastRepublicanElephant.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/NastRepublicanElephant.jpg/330px-NastRepublicanElephant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/NastRepublicanElephant.jpg/440px-NastRepublicanElephant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">1874 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nast" title="Thomas Nast">Nast</a> cartoon featuring the first notable appearance of the Republican elephant<sup id="cite_ref-harpweek_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harpweek-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Republicanlogo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Republicanlogo.svg/220px-Republicanlogo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="191" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Republicanlogo.svg/330px-Republicanlogo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Republicanlogo.svg/440px-Republicanlogo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="521" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The red, white and blue Republican <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a>, still a primary logo for many state GOP committees</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:GOP_Logo1.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ab/GOP_Logo1.svg/220px-GOP_Logo1.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ab/GOP_Logo1.svg/330px-GOP_Logo1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ab/GOP_Logo1.svg/440px-GOP_Logo1.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="198" data-file-height="127" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The circa 2013 GOP logo</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The party's founding members chose the name Republican Party in the mid-1850s as homage to the values of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republicanism</a> promoted by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Republican Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rutland_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutland-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The idea for the name came from an editorial by the party's leading publicist, Horace Greeley, who called for "some simple name like 'Republican' [that] would more fitly designate those who had united to restore the Union to its true mission of champion and promulgator of Liberty rather than propagandist of slavery".<sup id="cite_ref-ushistory_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ushistory-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The name reflects the 1776 republican values of civic virtue and opposition to aristocracy and corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-Gould2003p14_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gould2003p14-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> It is important to note that "republican" has a variety of meanings around the world and the Republican Party has evolved such that the meanings no longer always align.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Grand Old Party" is a traditional nickname for the Republican Party and the abbreviation "GOP" is a commonly used designation. The term originated in 1875 in the <i>Congressional Record</i>, referring to the party associated with the successful military defense of the Union as "this gallant old party". The following year in an article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Commercial_Tribune" title="Cincinnati Commercial Tribune">Cincinnati Commercial</a></i>, the term was modified to "grand old party". The first use of the abbreviation is dated 1884.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditional mascot of the party is the elephant. A political cartoon by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nast" title="Thomas Nast">Thomas Nast</a>, published in <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Magazine" title="Harper&#39;s Magazine">Harper's Weekly</a></i> on November 7, 1874, is considered the first important use of the symbol.<sup id="cite_ref-harpweek20031107_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harpweek20031107-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> An alternate symbol of the Republican Party in states such as <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> is the bald eagle as opposed to the Democratic rooster or the Democratic five-pointed star.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In Kentucky, the log cabin is a symbol of the Republican Party (not related to the gay Log Cabin Republicans organization).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally the party had no consistent color identity.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_presidential_election" title="2000 United States presidential election">2000 election</a>, the color <a href="/wiki/Political_color" class="mw-redirect" title="Political color">red became associated</a> with Republicans. During and after the election, the major broadcast networks used the same color scheme for the electoral map: states won by Republican nominee George W. Bush were colored red and states won by Democratic nominee Al Gore were colored blue. Due to the weeks-long <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_presidential_election_recount_in_Florida" title="2000 United States presidential election recount in Florida">dispute over the election results</a>, these color associations became firmly ingrained, persisting in subsequent years. Although the assignment of colors to political parties is unofficial and informal, the media has come to represent the respective political parties using these colors. The party and its candidates have also come to embrace the color red.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_organization">Structure and organization</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_United_States#Organization_of_American_political_parties" title="Politics of the United States">Politics of the United States §&#160;Organization of American political parties</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_state_and_territorial_Republican_Parties_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="List of state and territorial Republican Parties (United States)">List of state and territorial Republican Parties (United States)</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> (RNC) is responsible for promoting Republican campaign activities. It is responsible for developing and promoting the Republican political platform as well as coordinating fundraising and election strategy. Its current chairwoman is <a href="/wiki/Ronna_Romney_McDaniel" class="mw-redirect" title="Ronna Romney McDaniel">Ronna Romney McDaniel</a>. The chair of the RNC is chosen by the President when the Republicans have the White House or otherwise by the party's state committees.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Under the direction of the party's presidential candidate, the RNC supervises the <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Convention" title="Republican National Convention">Republican National Convention</a> (the highest body in the party) and raises funds for candidates. On the local level, there are similar state committees in every state and most large cities, counties and legislative districts, but they have far less money and influence than the national body.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The Republican House and Senate caucuses have separate fundraising and strategy committees. The <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Congressional_Committee" title="National Republican Congressional Committee">National Republican Congressional Committee</a> (NRCC) assists in House races while the <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Senatorial_Committee" title="National Republican Senatorial Committee">National Republican Senatorial Committee</a> (NRSC) does so in Senate races. They each raise over $100 million per election cycle and play important roles in recruiting strong state candidates while the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Governors_Association" title="Republican Governors Association">Republican Governors Association</a> (RGA) assists in state gubernatorial races. In 2016, it is chaired by Governor <a href="/wiki/Susana_Martinez" title="Susana Martinez">Susana Martinez</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rga-chair_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rga-chair-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Republican Party is a full member of the conservative <a href="/wiki/International_Democrat_Union" title="International Democrat Union">International Democrat Union</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Asia_Pacific_Democrat_Union" title="Asia Pacific Democrat Union">Asia Pacific Democrat Union</a>. It is also an associate member of the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Conservatives_and_Reformists_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Alliance of Conservatives and Reformists in Europe">Alliance of Conservatives and Reformists in Europe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-AECR_membership_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AECR_membership-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> which has close relations to the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> of the United Kingdom.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Positions">Positions</span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">This article is part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Category:Conservatism in the United States">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;background:#B0161E; padding-top:0.25em; font-size:150%; font-weight:normal; color:white; line-height:0.85em;"><a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States"><span style="color:#FFF">Conservatism in<br />the United States</span></a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><div class="noresize" style="height: 200px; width: 200px; "><map name="ImageMap_c8120dce3d049283"><area href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" shape="rect" coords="0,0,67,67" alt="Ronald Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan" /><area href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" shape="rect" coords="67,0,133,67" alt="Calvin Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge" /><area href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" shape="rect" coords="134,0,200,67" alt="Barry Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater" /><area href="/wiki/William_F._Buckley_Jr." shape="rect" coords="0,67,67,133" alt="William F. <a href="/wiki/File:DodgerBlue_flag_waving.svg" class="image"><img alt="DodgerBlue flag waving.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DodgerBlue_flag_waving.svg/15px-DodgerBlue_flag_waving.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DodgerBlue_flag_waving.svg/22px-DodgerBlue_flag_waving.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DodgerBlue_flag_waving.svg/30px-DodgerBlue_flag_waving.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="268" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Conservatism" title="Portal:Conservatism">Conservatism&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Conservatism_US" title="Template:Conservatism US"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Conservatism_US" title="Template talk:Conservatism US"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Conservatism_US&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_the_Republican_Party" title="Political positions of the Republican Party">Political positions of the Republican Party</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_policies">Economic policies</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calvin_Coolidge_cph.3g10777_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Calvin_Coolidge_cph.3g10777_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Calvin_Coolidge_cph.3g10777_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Calvin_Coolidge_cph.3g10777_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Calvin_Coolidge_cph.3g10777_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Calvin_Coolidge_cph.3g10777_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Calvin_Coolidge_cph.3g10777_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Calvin_Coolidge_cph.3g10777_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a>, 30th President of the United States (1923–1929)</div></div></div>
<p>Republicans strongly believe that <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free markets</a> and individual achievement are the primary factors behind economic prosperity. To this end, they advocate the elimination of government-run welfare programs in favor of private sector nonprofits and encouraging personal responsibility. Republicans also frequently advocate in favor of <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">fiscal conservatism</a> during Democratic administrations, but have shown themselves willing to increase federal debt when they are in charge of the government, such as the implementation of the Bush tax cuts, Medicare Part D and the Tax Cuts and Jobs Act of 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Despite pledges to roll back government spending, Republican administrations have since the late 1960s sustained previous levels of government spending.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern Republicans advocate the theory of <a href="/wiki/Supply_side_economics" class="mw-redirect" title="Supply side economics">supply side economics</a>, which holds that lower tax rates increase economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Many Republicans oppose <a href="/wiki/Progressive_taxation" class="mw-redirect" title="Progressive taxation">higher tax rates for higher earners</a>, which they believe are unfairly targeted at those who create jobs and wealth. They believe private spending is more efficient than government spending. Republican lawmakers have also sought to limit funding for tax enforcement and <a href="/wiki/Revenue_service" title="Revenue service">tax collection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-How_the_IRS_Was_Gutted_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_the_IRS_Was_Gutted-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> By 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a>, which Republicans had primarily starved of resources in the past decade, had lost considerable abilities to conduct audits and to engage in large-scale investigations of tax evasion.<sup id="cite_ref-How_the_IRS_Was_Gutted_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_the_IRS_Was_Gutted-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Republicans believe individuals should take responsibility for their own circumstances. They also believe the private sector is more effective in helping the poor through <a href="/wiki/Charity_(practice)" title="Charity (practice)">charity</a> than the government is through welfare programs and that social assistance programs often cause government dependency.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Republicans believe corporations should be able to establish their own employment practices, including benefits and wages, with the free market deciding the price of work. Since the 1920s, Republicans have generally been opposed by <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">labor union</a> organizations and members. At the national level, Republicans supported the <a href="/wiki/Taft-Hartley_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Taft-Hartley Act">Taft-Hartley Act</a> of 1947, which gives workers the right not to participate in unions. Modern Republicans at the state level generally support various <a href="/wiki/Right-to-work_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Right-to-work laws">right-to-work</a> laws, which prohibit <a href="/wiki/Union_security_agreement" title="Union security agreement">union security agreements</a> requiring all workers in a unionized workplace to pay dues or a fair-share fee, regardless of if they are members of the union or not.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Republicans tend to oppose increases in the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a>, believing that such increases hurt businesses by forcing them to cut and outsource jobs and pass costs along to consumers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The party opposes a <a href="/wiki/Single-payer_health_care" class="mw-redirect" title="Single-payer health care">single-payer health care</a> system, describing it as <a href="/wiki/Socialized_medicine" title="Socialized medicine">socialized medicine</a>.  The Republican Party has a mixed record of supporting the historically popular <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">Social Security</a>, Medicare and Medicaid programs.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_policies">Environmental policies</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_the_Republican_Party#Environmental_policies" title="Political positions of the Republican Party">Political positions of the Republican Party §&#160;Environmental policies</a></div>
<p>Historically, <a href="/wiki/Progressivism_in_the_United_States" title="Progressivism in the United States">progressive</a> leaders in the Republican Party supported <a href="/wiki/Environmental_protection" title="Environmental protection">environmental protection</a>. Republican President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> was a prominent <a href="/wiki/Conservation_(ethic)" title="Conservation (ethic)">conservationist</a> whose policies eventually led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Filler_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Filler-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> While Republican President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> was not an environmentalist, he signed legislation to create the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> in 1970 and had a comprehensive environmental program.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> However, this position has changed since the 1980s and the administration of President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, who labeled environmental regulations a burden on the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Dunlap_2010_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlap_2010-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Since then, Republicans have increasingly taken positions against environmental regulation, with some Republicans rejecting the scientific consensus on climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-Dunlap_2010_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlap_2010-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ringquist_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ringquist-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shipan_Environmental_Policy_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shipan_Environmental_Policy-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A._Schwarzenegger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/A._Schwarzenegger.jpg/170px-A._Schwarzenegger.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/A._Schwarzenegger.jpg/255px-A._Schwarzenegger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0f/A._Schwarzenegger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="380" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A._Schwarzenegger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_California" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Governors of California">38th</a> <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_California" title="Governor of California">Governor of California</a> (2003–2011)</div></div></div>
<p>In 2006, then-<a href="/wiki/Governor_of_California" title="Governor of California">California Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a> broke from Republican orthodoxy to sign several bills imposing caps on <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a> in California. Then-President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> opposed mandatory caps at a national level. Bush's decision not to regulate carbon dioxide as a pollutant was <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_v._Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Massachusetts v. Environmental Protection Agency">challenged in the Supreme Court by 12 states</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Landmark_Law_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Landmark_Law-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> with the court ruling against the Bush administration in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Bush also publicly opposed ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocols</a><sup id="cite_ref-Dunlap_2010_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlap_2010-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BushGW_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BushGW-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> which sought to limit greenhouse gas emissions and thereby combat climate change; his position was heavily criticized by climate scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
