<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_McCain_official_portrait_lightened.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/John_McCain_official_portrait_lightened.jpg/170px-John_McCain_official_portrait_lightened.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/John_McCain_official_portrait_lightened.jpg/255px-John_McCain_official_portrait_lightened.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/John_McCain_official_portrait_lightened.jpg/340px-John_McCain_official_portrait_lightened.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3040" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_McCain_official_portrait_lightened.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> (1987–2018)</div></div></div>
<p>The Republican Party rejects <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">cap-and-trade</a> policy to limit carbon emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In the 2000s, Senator <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a> proposed bills (such as the <a href="/wiki/McCain-Lieberman_Climate_Stewardship_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="McCain-Lieberman Climate Stewardship Act">McCain-Lieberman Climate Stewardship Act</a>) that would have regulated carbon emissions, but his position on climate change was unusual among high-ranking party members.<sup id="cite_ref-Dunlap_2010_107-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlap_2010-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Some Republican candidates have supported the development of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuels</a> in order to achieve <a href="/wiki/U.S._energy_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. energy independence">energy independence for the United States</a>.  Some Republicans support increased <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">oil drilling</a> in protected areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Arctic National Wildlife Refuge">Arctic National Wildlife Refuge</a>, a position that has drawn criticism from activists.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Republicans during the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Barack_Obama" title="Presidency of Barack Obama">presidency of Barack Obama</a> opposed his administration's new environmental regulations, such as those on carbon emissions from coal. In particular, many Republicans supported building the <a href="/wiki/Keystone_Pipeline" title="Keystone Pipeline">Keystone Pipeline</a>; this position was supported by businesses, but opposed by indigenous peoples' groups and environmental activists.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_American_Progress" title="Center for American Progress">Center for American Progress</a>, a non-profit liberal advocacy group, more than 55% of congressional Republicans were <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_denial" title="Climate change denial">climate change deniers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc20140512_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc20140512-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-charlestongazette20141222_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-charlestongazette20141222-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/PolitiFact" title="PolitiFact">PolitiFact</a> in May 2014 found "relatively few Republican members of Congress...accept the prevailing scientific conclusion that global warming is both real and man-made”. The group found eight members who acknowledged it, although the group acknowledged there could be more and that not all members of Congress have taken a stance on the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 2008 to 2017, the Republican Party went from "debating how to combat human-caused climate change to arguing that it does not exist", according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In January 2015, the Republican-led U.S. Senate voted 98–1 to pass a resolution acknowledging that "climate change is real and is not a hoax"; however, an amendment stating that "human activity significantly contributes to climate change" was supported by only five Republican senators.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_States" title="Immigration to the United States">Immigration to the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration_to_the_United_States" title="Illegal immigration to the United States">Illegal immigration to the United States</a></div>
<p>In the period 1850–1870, the Republican Party was more opposed to immigration than Democrats, in part because the Republican Party relied on the support of anti-Catholic and anti-immigrant parties, such as the <a href="/wiki/Know_Nothing" title="Know Nothing">Know-Nothings</a>, at the time. In the decades following the Civil War, the Republican Party grew more supportive of immigration, as it represented manufacturers in the Northeast (who wanted additional labor) whereas the Democratic Party came to be seen as the party of labor (which wanted fewer laborers to compete with). Starting in the 1970s, the parties switched places again, as the Democrats grew more supportive of immigration than Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Republicans are divided on how to confront <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration_to_the_United_States" title="Illegal immigration to the United States">illegal immigration</a> between a platform that allows for migrant workers and a path to citizenship for undocumented immigrants (supported by establishment types), versus a position focused on securing the border and deporting illegal immigrants (supported by populists). In 2006, the White House supported and Republican-led Senate passed <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Immigration_Reform_Act_of_2006" title="Comprehensive Immigration Reform Act of 2006">comprehensive immigration reform</a> that would eventually allow millions of illegal immigrants to become citizens, but the House (also led by Republicans) did not advance the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-Blanton_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blanton-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> After the defeat in the 2012 presidential election, particularly among Latinos, several Republicans advocated a friendlier approach to immigrants. However, in 2016 the field of candidates took a sharp position against illegal immigration, with leading candidate <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> proposing building a wall along the southern border. Proposals calling for immigration reform with a path to citizenship for undocumented immigrants have attracted broad Republican support in some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2016)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> polls. In a 2013 poll, 60% of Republicans supported the pathway concept.<sup id="cite_ref-long-past_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-long-past-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy_and_national_defense">Foreign policy and national defense</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_foreign_policy_and_national_defense_in_the_Republican_Party" title="History of foreign policy and national defense in the Republican Party">History of foreign policy and national defense in the Republican Party</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rumsfeld1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Rumsfeld1.jpg/170px-Rumsfeld1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Rumsfeld1.jpg/255px-Rumsfeld1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Rumsfeld1.jpg/340px-Rumsfeld1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rumsfeld1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Donald_Rumsfeld" title="Donald Rumsfeld">Donald Rumsfeld</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Secretaries_of_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="List of United States Secretaries of Defense">21st</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">United States Secretary of Defense</a> (2001–2006)</div></div></div>
<p>Some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2017)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in the Republican Party support <a href="/wiki/Unilateralism" title="Unilateralism">unilateralism</a> on issues of national security, believing in the ability and right of the United States to act without external support in matters of its national defense. In general, Republican thinking on defense and <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a> is heavily influenced by the theories of <a href="/wiki/Neorealism_(international_relations)" title="Neorealism (international relations)">neorealism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_realism" class="mw-redirect" title="Political realism">realism</a>, characterizing conflicts between nations as struggles between faceless forces of international structure as opposed to being the result of the ideas and actions of individual leaders. The realist school's influence shows in Reagan's <a href="/wiki/Evil_Empire_speech" title="Evil Empire speech">Evil Empire</a> stance on the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and George W. Bush's <a href="/wiki/Axis_of_evil" title="Axis of evil">Axis of evil</a> stance.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a>, many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2016)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in the party have supported <a href="/wiki/Neoconservatism" title="Neoconservatism">neoconservative</a> policies with regard to the War on Terror, including the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">2001 war in Afghanistan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a>. The George W. Bush administration took the position that the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Conventions</a> do not apply to <a href="/wiki/Unlawful_combatant" title="Unlawful combatant">unlawful combatants</a>, while other<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2016)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> prominent Republicans strongly oppose the use of enhanced interrogation techniques, which they view as torture.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Republicans have frequently advocated for restricting foreign aid as a means of asserting the national security and immigration interests of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Republican Party generally supports a strong alliance with <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and efforts to secure peace in the Middle East between Israel and its <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> neighbors.<sup id="cite_ref-Pro-Israel_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pro-Israel-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In recent years, Republicans have begun to move away from the <a href="/wiki/Two-state_solution" title="Two-state solution">two-state solution</a> approach to resolving the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In a 2014 poll, 59% of Republicans favored doing less abroad and focusing on the country's own problems instead.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2016 platform,<sup id="cite_ref-amazonaws1_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amazonaws1-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> the party's stance on the status of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> is: "We oppose any unilateral steps by either side to alter the status quo in the Taiwan Straits on the principle that all issues regarding the island's future must be resolved peacefully, through dialogue, and be agreeable to the people of Taiwan". In addition, if "China were to violate those principles, the United States, in accord with the Taiwan Relations Act, will help Taiwan defend itself".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_policies">Social policies</span></h3>
<p>The Republican Party is generally associated with <a href="/wiki/Social_conservative" class="mw-redirect" title="Social conservative">social conservative</a> policies, although it does have dissenting centrist and <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_in_the_United_States" title="Libertarianism in the United States">libertarian</a> factions. The social conservatives want laws that uphold their <a href="/wiki/Traditional_values" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional values">traditional values</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Opposition_to_same-sex_marriage_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Opposition to same-sex marriage in the United States">opposition to same-sex marriage</a>, abortion, and marijuana.<sup id="cite_ref-Zelizer_2004_704–5_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zelizer_2004_704–5-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Most conservative Republicans also oppose <a href="/wiki/Gun_control" title="Gun control">gun control</a>, <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration" title="Illegal immigration">illegal immigration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zelizer_2004_704–5_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zelizer_2004_704–5-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abortion_and_embryonic_stem_cell_research">Abortion and embryonic stem cell research</span></h4>
<p>A majority of the party's national and state candidates are <a href="/wiki/Pro-life" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro-life">pro-life</a> and oppose elective <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> on religious or moral grounds. While many advocate exceptions in the case of <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incest</a>, rape or the mother's life being at risk, in 2012 the party approved a platform advocating banning abortions without exception.<sup id="cite_ref-platform_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-platform-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> There were not highly polarized differences between the Democratic Party and the Republican Party prior to the <i><a href="/wiki/Roe_v._Wade" title="Roe v. Wade">Roe v. Wade</a></i> 1973 Supreme Court ruling (which made prohibitions on abortion rights unconstitutional), but after the Supreme Court ruling, opposition to abortion became an increasingly key national platform for the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Great_Divide:_Religious_and_Cultural_Conflict_in_American_Party_Politics2_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Great_Divide:_Religious_and_Cultural_Conflict_in_American_Party_Politics2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-How_race_and_religion_have_polarized_American_voters_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_race_and_religion_have_polarized_American_voters-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> As a result, Evangelicals gravitated towards the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Great_Divide:_Religious_and_Cultural_Conflict_in_American_Party_Politics2_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Great_Divide:_Religious_and_Cultural_Conflict_in_American_Party_Politics2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-How_race_and_religion_have_polarized_American_voters_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_race_and_religion_have_polarized_American_voters-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Republicans oppose government and tax-payer funding for abortion providers, notably <a href="/wiki/Planned_Parenthood" title="Planned Parenthood">Planned Parenthood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ontheissues_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ontheissues-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until its dissolution in 2018, <a href="/wiki/Republican_Majority_for_Choice" title="Republican Majority for Choice">Republican Majority for Choice</a>, a pro-choice PAC, advocated for amending the GOP platform to include pro-choice members.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Republicans have voted for increases in government funding of scientific research, members of the Republican Party actively oppose the federal funding of <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_stem_cell" title="Embryonic stem cell">embryonic stem cell</a> research beyond the original lines because it involves the destruction of human <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span></h4>
<p>Republicans are generally against <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a> for women and some minorities, often describing it as a "<a href="/wiki/Racial_quota" title="Racial quota">quota system</a>" and believing that it is not <a href="/wiki/Meritocratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Meritocratic">meritocratic</a> and that it is counter-productive socially by only further promoting <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a>. Many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2016)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Republicans support race-neutral admissions policies in universities, but support taking into account the socioeconomic status of the student.<sup id="cite_ref-affirmativeaction_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-affirmativeaction-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Eilperin_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eilperin-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gun_ownership">Gun ownership</span></h4><p>
Republicans generally support <a href="/wiki/Gun_politics_in_the_United_States" title="Gun politics in the United States">gun ownership rights</a> and oppose laws regulating guns.</p><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_(United_States)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2019</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Drugs">Drugs</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Illegal_drug_trade_in_the_United_States" title="Illegal drug trade in the United States">Illegal drug trade in the United States</a></div>
<p>Republicans have historically supported the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Drugs" class="mw-redirect" title="War on Drugs">War on Drugs</a> and oppose the <a href="/wiki/Legalization_of_drugs" class="mw-redirect" title="Legalization of drugs">legalization of drugs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> More recently, several<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2016)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> prominent Republicans have advocated for the reduction and reform of <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_sentencing" title="Mandatory sentencing">mandatory sentencing</a> laws with regards to drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="LGBT_issues">LGBT issues</span></h4>
<p>Owing largely to the prominence of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_right" title="Christian right">religious right</a> in conservative politics in the United States, the Republican Party had traditionally taken positions regarded as somewhat unwelcoming to the <a href="/wiki/Gay_rights_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Gay rights movement">gay rights movement</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">President Donald Trump</a> has expressed strong support for the LGBT community,<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> and the Republican party has begun shifting their views to express more support for <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Republicans have historically opposed <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>, while being divided on <a href="/wiki/Civil_union" title="Civil union">civil unions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Domestic_partnership" title="Domestic partnership">domestic partnerships</a>, with the issue being one that many believe helped <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> win re-election in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In both <a href="/wiki/108th_United_States_Congress" title="108th United States Congress">2004</a><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/109th_United_States_Congress" title="109th United States Congress">2006</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> President Bush, Senate Majority Leader <a href="/wiki/Bill_Frist" title="Bill Frist">Bill Frist</a>, and House Majority Leader <a href="/wiki/John_Boehner" title="John Boehner">John Boehner</a> promoted the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Marriage_Amendment" title="Federal Marriage Amendment">Federal Marriage Amendment</a>, a proposed constitutional amendment which would legally restrict the definition of marriage to heterosexual couples.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In both attempts, the amendment failed to secure enough votes to invoke <a href="/wiki/Cloture" title="Cloture">cloture</a> and thus ultimately was never passed. As more states legalized same-sex marriage in the 2010s, Republicans increasingly supported allowing each state to decide its own marriage policy.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Shifting_Landscape_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Shifting_Landscape-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> As of 2014, most state GOP platforms expressed opposition to same-sex marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> The 2016 <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">GOP Platform</a> included language supporting "traditional marriage" and condemning the Supreme Court's <a href="/wiki/Obergefell_v._Hodges" title="Obergefell v. Hodges">ruling</a> legalizing same-sex marriages.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> However, public opinion on this issue within the party has been changing.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Following his election as President of the United States in 2016, President-elect Donald Trump stated that he had no objection to same-sex marriage or to the Supreme Court decision in <i>Obergefell v. Hodges</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto7_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> As President, Donald Trump was the first sitting Republican president to recognize <a href="/wiki/Gay_pride" title="Gay pride">LGBT Pride Month</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Republican Party platform had opposed the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation_and_the_United_States_military" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexual orientation and the United States military">inclusion of gay people in the military</a> had opposed adding sexual orientation to the list of protected classes since 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The Republican Party opposed the inclusion of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_preference" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexual preference">sexual preference</a> in anti-discrimination statutes from 1992 to 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-1992_Republican_Party_platform_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1992_Republican_Party_platform-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The 2008 and 2012 Republican Party platform supported anti-discrimination statutes based on sex, race, age, religion, creed, disability, or national origin, but both platforms were silent on <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2012_Republican_Party_platform_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012_Republican_Party_platform-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2008_Republican_Party_platform_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Republican_Party_platform-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A group of LGBT Republicans is the <a href="/wiki/Log_Cabin_Republicans" title="Log Cabin Republicans">Log Cabin Republicans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Voting_rights">Voting rights</span></h4>
<p>Virtually all restrictions on voting have in recent years been implemented by Republicans. Republicans, mainly at the state level, argue that the restrictions (such as purging voter rolls, limiting voting locations and prosecuting double voting) are vital to prevent <a href="/wiki/Voter_fraud" class="mw-redirect" title="Voter fraud">voter fraud</a>, claiming that voter fraud is an underestimated issue in elections. However, research has indicated that voter fraud is very uncommon, as civil and voting rights organizations often accuse Republicans of enacting restrictions to influence elections in the party's favor. Many laws or regulations restricting voting enacted by Republicans have been successfully challenged in court, with court rulings striking down such regulations and accusing Republicans of establishing them with partisan purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Democracy">Democracy</span></h4>
<table class="box-POV plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" decoding="async" width="45" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a> of this section is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">disputed</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Republican_Party_(United_States)##" title="Talk:Republican Party (United States)">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until <a href="/wiki/Template:POV#When_to_remove" title="Template:POV">conditions to do so are met</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Towards the end of the 1990s and in the early 21st century, the Republican Party increasingly resorted to "<a href="/wiki/Constitutional_hardball" title="Constitutional hardball">constitutional hardball</a>" practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Glassman_2018_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glassman_2018-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of scholars have stated that the House speakership of Republican Newt Gingrich played a key role in undermining democratic norms in the United States, hastening political polarization, and increasing partisan prejudice.<sup id="cite_ref-Uncivil_Agreement_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uncivil_Agreement-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Polarizers_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Polarizers-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Gingrich_Senators:_The_Roots_of_Partisan_Warfare_in_Congress_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Gingrich_Senators:_The_Roots_of_Partisan_Warfare_in_Congress-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-How_Democracies_Die_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Democracies_Die-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-American_Amnesia_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-American_Amnesia-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Appropriations_to_the_Extreme:_Partisanship_and_the_Power_of_the_Purse_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appropriations_to_the_Extreme:_Partisanship_and_the_Power_of_the_Purse-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Let&#39;s_Play_Hardball_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Let&#39;s_Play_Hardball-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asymmetrical_Constitutional_Hardball_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asymmetrical_Constitutional_Hardball-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Red_Fighting_Blue_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Red_Fighting_Blue-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> According to Harvard University political scientists Daniel Ziblatt and Steven Levitsky, Gingrich's speakership had a profound and lasting impact on American politics and health of American democracy. They argue that Gingrich instilled a "combative" approach in the Republican Party, where hateful language and hyper-partisanship became commonplace, and where democratic norms were abandoned. Gingrich frequently questioned the patriotism of Democrats, called them corrupt, compared them to fascists, and accused them of wanting to destroy the United States. Gingrich was also involved in several major government shutdowns.<sup id="cite_ref-How_Democracies_Die_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Democracies_Die-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars have also characterized Mitch McConnell's tenure as Senate Minority Leader and Senate Majority Leader during the Obama presidency as one where obstructionism reached all-time highs.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Senate_Syndrome:_The_Evolution_of_Procedural_Warfare_in_the_Modern_U.S._Senate_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Senate_Syndrome:_The_Evolution_of_Procedural_Warfare_in_the_Modern_U.S._Senate-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Political scientists have referred to McConnell's use of the filibuster as "constitutional hardball", referring to the misuse of procedural tools in a way that undermines democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-Glassman_2018_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glassman_2018-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-How_Democracies_Die_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Democracies_Die-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-American_Amnesia_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-American_Amnesia-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asymmetrical_Constitutional_Hardball_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asymmetrical_Constitutional_Hardball-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> McConnell delayed and obstructed health care reform and banking reform, which were two landmark pieces of legislation that Democrats sought to pass (and in fact did pass<sup id="cite_ref-auto5_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto5_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>) early in Obama's tenure.<sup id="cite_ref-Party_and_Procedure_in_the_United_States_Congress,_Second_Edition_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Party_and_Procedure_in_the_United_States_Congress,_Second_Edition-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-What_the_Filibuster_Tells_Us_About_the_Senate_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_Filibuster_Tells_Us_About_the_Senate-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> By delaying Democratic priority legislation, McConnell stymied the output of Congress. Political scientists Eric Schickler and Gregory J. Wawro write, "by slowing action even on measures supported by many Republicans, McConnell capitalized on the scarcity of floor time, forcing Democratic leaders into difficult trade-offs concerning which measures were worth pursuing. That is, given that Democrats had just two years with sizeable majorities to enact as much of their agenda as possible, slowing the Senate’s ability to process even routine measures limited the sheer volume of liberal bills that could be adopted."<sup id="cite_ref-What_the_Filibuster_Tells_Us_About_the_Senate_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_Filibuster_Tells_Us_About_the_Senate-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>McConnell's refusal to hold hearings on Supreme Court nominee <a href="/wiki/Merrick_Garland" title="Merrick Garland">Merrick Garland</a> during the final year of Obama's presidency was described by political scientists and legal scholars as "unprecedented",<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> a "culmination of this confrontational style,"<sup id="cite_ref-The_Obama_Presidency_and_the_Politics_of_Change_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Obama_Presidency_and_the_Politics_of_Change-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> a "blatant abuse of constitutional norms,"<sup id="cite_ref-The_People_vs._Democracy_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_People_vs._Democracy-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> and a "classic example of constitutional hardball."<sup id="cite_ref-Asymmetrical_Constitutional_Hardball_188-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asymmetrical_Constitutional_Hardball-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>  Senate Republicans justified this move by pointing to a 1992 speech from then-Senate Judiciary Committee Chair <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Joe_Biden_Argued_for_Delaying_Supreme_Court_Picks_in_1992_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joe_Biden_Argued_for_Delaying_Supreme_Court_Picks_in_1992-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> in that speech, Biden argued that hearings on any potential Supreme Court nominee that year should be postponed until after Election Day.<sup id="cite_ref-Joe_Biden_Argued_for_Delaying_Supreme_Court_Picks_in_1992_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joe_Biden_Argued_for_Delaying_Supreme_Court_Picks_in_1992-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Biden_in_’92:_No_election-season_Supreme_Court_nominees_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biden_in_’92:_No_election-season_Supreme_Court_nominees-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Biden contested this interpretation of his 1992 speech.<sup id="cite_ref-Biden_in_’92:_No_election-season_Supreme_Court_nominees_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biden_in_’92:_No_election-season_Supreme_Court_nominees-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2004_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/2004_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg/280px-2004_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/2004_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg/420px-2004_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/2004_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg/560px-2004_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="555" data-file-height="352" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2004_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This map shows the vote in the <a href="/wiki/2004_United_States_presidential_election" title="2004 United States presidential election">2004 presidential election</a> by county.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;A&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2016_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/2016_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg/280px-2016_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/2016_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg/420px-2016_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/2016_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg/560px-2016_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="990" data-file-height="627" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2016_Presidential_Election_by_County.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This map shows the vote in the <a href="/wiki/2016_United_States_presidential_election" title="2016 United States presidential election">2016 presidential election</a> by county.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;B&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the Party's infancy, its base consisted of Northern white Protestants and African Americans nationwide. Its first presidential candidate, <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a>, received almost no votes in the South. This trend continued into the 20th century. Following the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a>, the Southern states became more reliably Republican in presidential politics, while Northeastern states became more reliably Democratic.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Regional_Variations_in_the_Realignment_of_American_Politics,_1944–2004_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Regional_Variations_in_the_Realignment_of_American_Politics,_1944–2004-207">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Issue_Evolution_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Issue_Evolution-212">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Studies show that Southern whites shifted to the Republican Party due to racial conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-Issue_Evolution_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Issue_Evolution-212">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Party's 21st-century base consists of groups such as white, married Protestants, rural and suburban citizens and non-union workers without college degrees, with urban residents, ethnic minorities, the unmarried and union workers having shifted to the Democratic Party.<sup id="cite_ref-voter_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-voter-216">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2015 <a href="/wiki/The_Gallup_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="The Gallup Organization">Gallup poll</a>, 25% of Americans identify as Republican and 16% identify as leaning Republican. In comparison, 30% identify as Democratic and 16% identify as leaning Democratic. The Democratic Party has typically held an overall edge in party identification since Gallup began polling on the issue in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> noted that the Republican Party was strong in the South, the Great Plains, and the Mountain States.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> The 21st century Republican Party also draws strength from rural areas of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology_and_factions">Ideology and factions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Factions_in_the_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Factions in the Republican Party (United States)">Factions in the Republican Party (United States)</a></div>
<p>In a 2015 Gallup poll, 42% of Republicans and Republican-leaning independents identified as economically and socially conservative, followed by 24% as socially and economically moderate or liberal, 20% as socially moderate or liberal and fiscally conservative and 10% as socially conservative and fiscally moderate or liberal.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> When ideology is separated into social and economic issues, a 2015 <i>Gallup</i> poll found that 53% of Republicans called themselves "<a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">socially conservative</a>", 34% chose the label "<a href="/wiki/Socially_moderate" class="mw-redirect" title="Socially moderate">socially moderate</a>", and 11% called themselves "<a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">socially liberal</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-On_Social_Ideology,_the_Left_Catches_Up_to_the_Right_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-On_Social_Ideology,_the_Left_Catches_Up_to_the_Right-221">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> On economic issues, the same 2015 poll revealed that 64% of Republicans chose the label "economic <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">conservative</a>" to describe their views on fiscal policy, while 27% selected the label "economic moderate" and 7% opted for the "economic liberal" label.<sup id="cite_ref-On_Social_Ideology,_the_Left_Catches_Up_to_the_Right_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-On_Social_Ideology,_the_Left_Catches_Up_to_the_Right-221">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern Republican Party includes <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservatives</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Paul_Gottfried_2009_p._12_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Gottfried_2009_p._12-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centrists</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiscal conservatism in the United States">fiscal conservatives</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Fiscal_Conservatism_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fiscal_Conservatism-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_in_the_United_States" title="Libertarianism in the United States">libertarians</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-gopfuture_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gopfuture-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neoconservatism" title="Neoconservatism">neoconservatives</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-gopfuture_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gopfuture-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paleoconservatism" title="Paleoconservatism">paleoconservatives</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-paleo_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paleo-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_populism_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Right-wing populism in the United States">right-wing populists</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Social conservatism in the United States">social conservatives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> In 2018, <i><a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a></i> polling found that 69% of Republicans described themselves as "<a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a>", while 25% opted for the term "moderate" and another 5% self-identified as "<a href="/wiki/American_Liberalism" class="mw-redirect" title="American Liberalism">liberal</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ron_Paul,_official_Congressional_photo_portrait,_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ron_Paul%2C_official_Congressional_photo_portrait%2C_2007.jpg/170px-Ron_Paul%2C_official_Congressional_photo_portrait%2C_2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ron_Paul%2C_official_Congressional_photo_portrait%2C_2007.jpg/255px-Ron_Paul%2C_official_Congressional_photo_portrait%2C_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ron_Paul%2C_official_Congressional_photo_portrait%2C_2007.jpg/340px-Ron_Paul%2C_official_Congressional_photo_portrait%2C_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1189" data-file-height="1491" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ron_Paul,_official_Congressional_photo_portrait,_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul" title="Ron Paul">Ron Paul</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States Representative</a> from <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> (1997–2013)</div></div></div>
<p>In addition to splits over ideology, the 21st-century Republican Party can be broadly divided into establishment and anti-establishment wings.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Nationwide polls of Republican voters in 2014 by the Pew Center identified a growing split in the Republican coalition, between "business conservatives" or "establishment conservatives" on one side and "steadfast conservatives" or "populist conservatives" on the other.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Talk_radio">Talk radio</span></h3>
<p>In the 21st century, conservatives on <a href="/wiki/Talk_radio" title="Talk radio">talk radio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a>, as well as such outlets as the <a href="/wiki/Drudge_Report" title="Drudge Report">Drudge Report</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breitbart_News" title="Breitbart News">Breitbart News</a>,  became a powerful influence on shaping the information received, and the judgments made by both rank-and-file Republicans and President Trump.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> They include <a href="/wiki/Rush_Limbaugh" title="Rush Limbaugh">Rush Limbaugh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sean_Hannity" title="Sean Hannity">Sean Hannity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Elder" title="Larry Elder">Larry Elder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Beck" title="Glenn Beck">Glenn Beck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Levin" title="Mark Levin">Mark Levin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dana_Loesch" title="Dana Loesch">Dana Loesch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Hewitt" title="Hugh Hewitt">Hugh Hewitt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Gallagher_(political_commentator)" title="Mike Gallagher (political commentator)">Mike Gallagher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neal_Boortz" title="Neal Boortz">Neal Boortz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laura_Ingraham" title="Laura Ingraham">Laura Ingraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Prager" title="Dennis Prager">Dennis Prager</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Reagan" title="Michael Reagan">Michael Reagan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Howie_Carr" title="Howie Carr">Howie Carr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Savage_(commentator)" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael Savage (commentator)">Michael Savage</a>, as well as many local commentators who support Republican causes while vocally opposing the left.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_community">Business community</span></h3>
<p>The Republican Party has traditionally been a pro-business party. It garners major support from a wide variety of industries from the financial sector to small businesses. Republicans are about 50 percent more likely to be self-employed and are more likely to work in management.<sup id="cite_ref-Fried2008p104_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fried2008p104-233">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>A survey cited by <i>The Washington Post</i> in 2012 stated that 61 percent of small business owners planned to vote for Republican presidential candidate Mitt Romney. Small business became a major theme of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Republican_National_Convention" title="2012 Republican National Convention">2012 Republican National Convention</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h3>
<p>The Democrats do better among younger Americans and Republicans among older Americans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 2006, Republicans won 38% of the voters aged 18–29.<sup id="cite_ref-2006cnnexitpolls_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2006cnnexitpolls-235">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Low-income voters tend to favor the Democrats<sup id="cite_ref-auto6_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-236">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> while high-income voters tend to support the Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-auto6_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-236">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, Obama won 60% of voters with income under $50,000 and 45% of those with incomes higher than that.<sup id="cite_ref-2012_Exit_Poll_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012_Exit_Poll-237">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> Bush won 41% of the poorest 20% of voters in 2004, 55% of the richest twenty percent and 53% of those in between. In the 2006 House races, the voters with incomes over $50,000 were 49% Republican while those with incomes under that amount were 38% Republican.<sup id="cite_ref-2006cnnexitpolls_235-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2006cnnexitpolls-235">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span></h4>
<p>Since 1980, a "gender gap" has seen stronger support for the Republican Party among men than among women. In 2012, Obama won 55% of the women and 45% of the men—and more women voted than men.<sup id="cite_ref-2012_Exit_Poll_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012_Exit_Poll-237">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> In the 2006 House races, 43% of women voted Republican while 47% of men did so.<sup id="cite_ref-2006cnnexitpolls_235-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2006cnnexitpolls-235">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> In the 2010 midterms, the "gender gap" was reduced with women supporting Republican and Democratic candidates equally 49% to 49%.<sup id="cite_ref-abcnews3775_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews3775-238">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weeks2010_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks2010-239">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Unmarried and divorced women were far more likely to vote for <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-wvwv2004_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wvwv2004-240">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The 2012 returns revealed a continued weakness among unmarried women for the GOP, a large and growing portion of the electorate.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Although women supported Obama over <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a> by a margin of 55–44% in 2012, Romney prevailed amongst married women, 53–46%.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Obama won unmarried women 67–31%.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h4>
<p>In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> conducted a study of registered voters with a 35–28, Democrat-to-Republican gap. They found that self-described Democrats had a +8 advantage over Republicans among college graduates, +14 of all post-graduates polled. Republicans were +11 among white men with college degrees, Democrats +10 among women with degrees. Democrats accounted for 36% of all respondents with an education of high school or less and Republicans were 28%. When isolating just white registered voters polled, Republicans had a +6 advantage overall and were +9 of those with a high school education or less.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2012_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2012-244">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Following the 2016 presidential election, exit polls indicated that "Donald Trump attracted a large share of the vote from whites without a college degree, receiving 72 percent of the white non-college male vote and 62 percent of the white non-college female vote". Overall, 52% of voters with college degrees voted for Hillary Clinton in 2016, while 52% of voters without college degrees voted for Trump.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnicity">Ethnicity</span></h4>
<p>Republicans have been winning under 15% of the black vote in recent national elections (1980 to 2016). The party abolished slavery under <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, defeated the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a> and gave blacks the legal right to vote during <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction in the late 1860s</a>. Until the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> of the 1930s, blacks supported the Republican Party by large margins.<sup id="cite_ref-South_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South-246">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Black voters shifted to the Democratic Party beginning in the 1930s, when major Democratic figures such as Eleanor Roosevelt began to support civil rights and the New Deal offered them employment opportunities. They became one of the core components of the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_coalition" title="New Deal coalition">New Deal coalition</a>. In the South, after the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Voting Rights Act">Voting Rights Act</a> to prohibit racial discrimination in elections was passed by a bipartisan coalition in 1965, blacks were able to vote again and ever since have formed a significant portion (20–50%) of the Democratic vote in that region.<sup id="cite_ref-Sitkoff_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sitkoff-247">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2010 elections, two African-American Republicans--<a href="/wiki/Tim_Scott" title="Tim Scott">Tim Scott</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allen_West_(politician)" title="Allen West (politician)">Allen West</a>—were elected to the House of Representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Holmes2010_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes2010-248">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In recent decades, Republicans have been moderately successful in gaining support from <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asian_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian American">Asian American</a> voters. George W. Bush, who campaigned energetically for Hispanic votes, received 35% of their vote in 2000 and 44% in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-2004cnnexitpolls_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2004cnnexitpolls-249">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> The party's strong anti-communist stance has made it popular among some minority groups from current and former Communist states, in particular <a href="/wiki/Cuban_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuban American">Cuban Americans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean American">Korean Americans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese American">Chinese Americans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Vietnamese American">Vietnamese Americans</a>. The 2007 election of <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Jindal" title="Bobby Jindal">Bobby Jindal</a> as Governor of Louisiana was hailed as pathbreaking.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC7412_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC7412-250">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Jindal became the first elected minority governor in <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and the first state governor of <a href="/wiki/Non-resident_Indian_and_person_of_Indian_origin" title="Non-resident Indian and person of Indian origin">Indian</a> descent.<sup id="cite_ref-deccanherald_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deccanherald-251">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/John_Avlon" title="John Avlon">John Avlon</a>, in 2013, the Republican party was more ethnically diverse at the statewide elected official level than the Democratic Party was; GOP statewide elected officials included Latino Nevada Governor <a href="/wiki/Brian_Sandoval" title="Brian Sandoval">Brian Sandoval</a> and African-American U.S. senator <a href="/wiki/Tim_Scott" title="Tim Scott">Tim Scott</a> of South Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, 88% of Romney voters were white while 56% of Obama voters were white.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> In the 2008 presidential election, John McCain won 55% of white votes, 35% of Asian votes, 31% of Hispanic votes and 4% of African American votes.<sup id="cite_ref-pewresearch_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewresearch-254">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In the 2010 House election, Republicans won 60% of the white votes, 38% of Hispanic votes and 9% of the African American vote.<sup id="cite_ref-pewresearch1790_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewresearch1790-255">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2019, Republican candidates had lost the popular vote in six out of the last seven presidential elections.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Demographers have pointed to the steady decline (as a percentage of the eligible voters) of its core base of older, less educated men.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_beliefs">Religious beliefs</span></h4>
<p>Religion has always played a major role for both parties, but in the course of a century, the parties' religious compositions have changed. Religion was a major dividing line between the parties before <a href="/wiki/1960_United_States_presidential_election" title="1960 United States presidential election">1960</a>, with Catholics, Jews, and Southern Protestants heavily Democratic and Northeastern Protestants heavily Republican. Most of the old differences faded away after the realignment of the 1970s and 1980s that undercut the New Deal coalition.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Voters who attend church weekly gave 61% of their votes to Bush in <a href="/wiki/2004_United_States_presidential_election" title="2004 United States presidential election">2004</a> and those who attend occasionally gave him only 47% while those who never attend gave him 36%. Fifty-nine percent of Protestants voted for Bush, along with 52% of Catholics (even though <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> was Catholic). Since 1980, large majorities of <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">evangelicals</a> have voted Republican; 70–80% voted for Bush in 2000 and 2004 and 70% for Republican House candidates in <a href="/wiki/United_States_general_elections,_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="United States general elections, 2006">2006</a>. Jews continue to vote 70–80% Democratic. Democrats have close links with the African American churches, especially the <a href="/wiki/National_Baptist_Convention,_USA,_Inc." title="National Baptist Convention, USA, Inc.">National Baptists</a>, while their historic dominance among Catholic voters has eroded to 54–46 in the 2010 midterms.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-262">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> The main line traditional Protestants (Methodists, Lutherans, Presbyterians, Episcopalians and Disciples) have dropped to about 55% Republican (in contrast to 75% before 1968). The mainline denominations are rapidly shrinking in size. Members of the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-Day Saints in Utah and neighboring states voted 75% or more for Bush in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Catholic Republican leaders try to stay in line with the teachings of the Catholic Church on subjects such as abortion, euthanasia, embryonic stem cell research and same-sex marriage, they differ on the death penalty and contraception.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a>' 2015 encyclical <i><a href="/wiki/Laudato_si%27" title="Laudato si&#39;">Laudato si'</a></i> sparked a discussion on the positions of Catholic Republicans in relation to the positions of the Church. The Pope's encyclical on behalf of the Catholic Church officially acknowledges a man-made climate change caused by burning fossil fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> The Pope says the warming of the planet is rooted in a throwaway culture and the developed world's indifference to the destruction of the planet in pursuit of short-term economic gains. According to <i>The New York Times</i>, <i>Laudato si'</i> put pressure on the Catholic candidates in the 2016 election: <a href="/wiki/Jeb_Bush" title="Jeb Bush">Jeb Bush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Jindal" title="Bobby Jindal">Bobby Jindal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marco_Rubio" title="Marco Rubio">Marco Rubio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rick_Santorum" title="Rick Santorum">Rick Santorum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-davenport_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davenport-266">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> With leading Democrats praising the encyclical, James Bretzke, a professor of moral theology at Boston College, has said that both sides were being disingenuous: "I think it shows that both the Republicans and the Democrats... like to use religious authority and, in this case, the Pope to support positions they have arrived at independently... There is a certain insincerity, a hypocrisy I think, on both sides".<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> While a Pew Research poll indicates Catholics are more likely to believe the Earth is warming than non-Catholics, 51% of Catholic Republicans believe in global warming (less than the general population) and only 24% of Catholic Republicans believe global warming is caused by human activity.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_presidents">Republican presidents</span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-died-269"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-died_269-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-died_269-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-died_269-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-died_269-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Died in office.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-270"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-270">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Resigned from office.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: upper-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All major Republican geographic constituencies are visible: <a href="/wiki/Red_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Red state">red</a> dominates the map—showing Republican strength in the rural areas—while the denser areas (i.e. cities) are <a href="/wiki/Blue_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue state">blue</a>. Notable exceptions include the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_states" title="Pacific states">Pacific coast</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Belt_(U.S._region)" title="Black Belt (U.S. region)">Black Belt</a>, areas with high <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> populations and the <a href="/wiki/File:New_2000_hispanic_percent.gif" title="File:New 2000 hispanic percent.gif">heavily Hispanic</a> parts of the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">Southwest</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-205"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-205">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Similar to the 2004 map, Republicans dominate in rural areas, making improvements in the <a href="/wiki/Appalachia" title="Appalachia">Appalachian</a> states, namely <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, where the party won all but two counties; and <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, where every county in the state voted Republican. The party also improved in many rural counties in <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwestern</a> states. Contrarily, the party suffered substantial losses in urbanized areas such <a href="/wiki/Dallas_County,_Texas" title="Dallas County, Texas">Dallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harris_County,_Texas" title="Harris County, Texas">Harris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fort_Bend_County,_Texas" title="Fort Bend County, Texas">Fort Bend</a> counties in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">Orange</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_County" class="mw-redirect" title="San Diego County">San Diego</a> counties in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, all of which were won in 2004, but lost in 2016</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-272"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-272">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Hayes won a majority of votes in the Electoral College, Democrat <a href="/wiki/Samuel_J._Tilden" title="Samuel J. Tilden">Samuel J. Tilden</a> won a majority of the popular vote.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-273"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-273">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Harrison won a majority of votes in the Electoral College, Democrat <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> won a plurality of the popular vote.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-274"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-274">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Bush won a majority of votes in the Electoral College, Democrat <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a> won a plurality of the popular vote.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-275"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-275">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Trump won a majority of votes in the Electoral College, Democrat <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> won a plurality of the popular vote.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
